			<h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading">Taiwan</h1>
			<!-- /firstHeading -->
			<!-- bodyContent -->
			<div id="bodyContent">
								<!-- tagline -->
				<div id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
				<!-- /tagline -->

				<div lang="en" dir="ltr" class="mw-content-ltr"><div class="dablink">"Formosa" redirects here. For other uses, see <a href="/wiki/Formosa_(disambiguation)" title="Formosa (disambiguation)">Formosa (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<div class="dablink">This article is about the island.  For the state governing it which is also commonly called "Taiwan", see <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_China" title="Republic of China">Republic of China</a>.  For other uses, see <a href="/wiki/Taiwan_(disambiguation)" title="Taiwan (disambiguation)">Taiwan (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<div class="metadata topicon" id="protected-icon" style="display:none; right:55px;"><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Protection_policy#semi" title="This article is semi-protected."><img alt="Page semi-protected" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/Padlock-silver.svg/20px-Padlock-silver.svg.png" width="20" height="20" /></a></div>
<table class="infobox vcard" cellspacing="5" style="width:22em;">
<caption class="fn org" style="">Taiwan<br />
<span lang="zh-tw" xml:lang="zh-tw">臺灣</span><br />
<span lang="zh-tw" xml:lang="zh-tw">台灣</span></caption>
<tr class="">
<td colspan="2" class="" style="text-align:center;"><a href="/wiki/File:Taiwan_NASA_Terra_MODIS_23791.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/Taiwan_NASA_Terra_MODIS_23791.jpg/200px-Taiwan_NASA_Terra_MODIS_23791.jpg" width="200" height="267" /></a><br />
<span style="">Taiwan is mostly mountainous in the east, with gently sloping plains in the west. The <a href="/wiki/Penghu_Islands" title="Penghu Islands" class="mw-redirect">Penghu Islands</a> are west of Taiwan.</span></td>
</tr>

</td>

style="border-bottom:1px dotted">ˈ</span></a></span><span class="IPA"><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:IPA_for_English#Key" title="Wikipedia:IPA for English"><span title="'w' in 'wind'" style="border-bottom:1px dotted">w</span></a></span><span class="IPA"><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:IPA_for_English#Key" title="Wikipedia:IPA for English"><span title="'a' in 'father'" style="border-bottom:1px dotted">ɑː</span></a></span><span class="IPA"><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:IPA_for_English#Key" title="Wikipedia:IPA for English"><span title="'n' in 'nigh'" style="border-bottom:1px dotted">n</span></a></span><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA"><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:IPA_for_English" title="Wikipedia:IPA for English">/</a></span></span> <span title="English pronunciation respelling" class="IPA"><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Pronunciation_respelling_key" title="Wikipedia:Pronunciation respelling key"><i><b><span style="font-variant:small-caps;">ty-wahn</span></b></i></a></span>),<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5"><span>[</span>6<span>]</span></a></sup> also known, especially in the past, as <b>Formosa</b> (from <a href="/wiki/Portuguese_language" title="Portuguese language">Portuguese</a>: <span lang="pt" xml:lang="pt"><i>Ilha Formosa</i></span>, "Beautiful Island"), is the largest island of the <a href="/wiki/List_of_islands_of_the_Republic_of_China#Taiwan_and_surrounding_Islands" title="List of islands of the Republic of China">same-named island group</a> of <a href="/wiki/East_Asia" title="East Asia">East Asia</a> in the western <a href="/wiki/Pacific_Ocean" title="Pacific Ocean">Pacific Ocean</a> and located off the southeastern coast of <a href="/wiki/Mainland_China" title="Mainland China">mainland China</a>. The island forms over 99% of the current territory of the <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_China" title="Republic of China">Republic of China</a> (ROC) following the <a href="/wiki/Chinese_Civil_War" title="Chinese Civil War">Chinese Civil War</a> in 1950. Due to this reason and the <a href="/wiki/Political_status_of_Taiwan" title="Political status of Taiwan">disputed political status of Republic of China</a> in the United Nations, the name "Taiwan" has also become the <i><a href="/wiki/Pars_pro_toto" title="Pars pro toto">pars pro toto</a></i> common name for the country itself.<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6"><span>[</span>7<span>]</span></a></sup> (See also <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_China#Names" title="Republic of China">Names of the Republic of China</a>.)</p>
<p>Separated from the <a href="/wiki/Asia" title="Asia">Asian continent</a> by the 160&#160;km (99&#160;mi) wide <a href="/wiki/Taiwan_Strait" title="Taiwan Strait">Taiwan Strait</a>, Taiwan is 394&#160;km (245&#160;mi) long and 144 kilometres (89&#160;mi) wide.<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7"><span>[</span>8<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8"><span>[</span>9<span>]</span></a></sup> To the northeast are the <a href="/wiki/Japanese_archipelago" title="Japanese archipelago">main islands of Japan</a> and the <a href="/wiki/East_China_Sea" title="East China Sea">East China Sea</a>, and the southern end of the <a href="/wiki/Ryukyu_Islands" title="Ryukyu Islands">Ryukyu Islands</a> of Japan is directly to the east; the <a href="/wiki/Batanes_Islands" title="Batanes Islands" class="mw-redirect">Batanes Islands</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Philippines" title="Philippines">Philippines</a> lie to its south across the <a href="/wiki/Bashi_Channel" title="Bashi Channel">Bashi Channel</a>. The mountainous island spans the <a href="/wiki/Tropic_of_Cancer" title="Tropic of Cancer">Tropic of Cancer</a> and is covered by <a href="/wiki/Tropical" title="Tropical" class="mw-redirect">tropical</a> and <a href="/wiki/Humid_subtropical_climate" title="Humid subtropical climate">subtropical</a> vegetation. Other <a href="/wiki/List_of_islands_of_the_Republic_of_China#Taiwan" title="List of islands of the Republic of China">minor islands and islets of the group</a> include the <a href="/wiki/Penghu_Islands" title="Penghu Islands" class="mw-redirect">Penghu Islands (Pescadores)</a>, <a href="/wiki/Green_Island,_Taiwan" title="Green Island, Taiwan">Green Island</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Orchid_Island" title="Orchid Island">Orchid Island</a>, as well as the <a href="/wiki/Senkaku_Islands" title="Senkaku Islands">Diaoyutai Islands</a> (Senkaku islands), which have been controlled by Japan since the 1970s.</p>
<p>Taiwan was ceded to the <a href="/wiki/Empire_of_Japan" title="Empire of Japan">Empire of Japan</a> by the <a href="/wiki/Qing_Empire" title="Qing Empire" class="mw-redirect">Qing Empire</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Shimonoseki" title="Treaty of Shimonoseki">Treaty of Shimonoseki</a> after the <a href="/wiki/First_Sino-Japanese_War" title="First Sino-Japanese War">First Sino-Japanese War</a> in 1895. In 1945 the Republic of China acquired control of Taiwan from Japan as a result of <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>. Four years later the ROC lost <a href="/wiki/Mainland_China" title="Mainland China">mainland China</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Chinese_Civil_War" title="Chinese Civil War">Chinese Civil War</a> to the <a href="/wiki/Communist_Party_of_China" title="Communist Party of China">Communist Party of China</a> and resettled its government to Taiwan. Taiwan composes the vast majority of the ROC's territory since 1950, and this is one of multiple reasons that the ROC is commonly known as "Taiwan". The <a href="/wiki/Political_status_of_Taiwan" title="Political status of Taiwan">political status of Taiwan</a> is disputed because it is claimed by the <a href="/wiki/People%27s_Republic_of_China" title="People's Republic of China" class="mw-redirect">People's Republic of China</a>, which was established in 1949 by the communists on mainland China and considers itself the <a href="/wiki/Successor_state" title="Successor state" class="mw-redirect">successor state</a> to the ROC.<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9"><span>[</span>10<span>]</span></a></sup> In fact, since PRC's establishment, it never controlled any of the territories the ROC government currently governs. Japan had originally acquired Taiwan from the Qing Empire in 1895 under Article 2 of the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Shimonoseki" title="Treaty of Shimonoseki">Treaty of Shimonoseki</a>. At the end of World War II, Japan renounced all claims to sovereignty over its former colonial possessions, including Taiwan and Penghu (Pescadores),<sup id="cite_ref-sankeishimbun20090728_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sankeishimbun20090728-10"><span>[</span>11<span>]</span></a></sup> but did not specify to whom Taiwan and Penghu should be assigned. This fact and subsequent handling of Taiwan's <a href="/wiki/Sovereignty" title="Sovereignty">sovereignty</a> by the <a href="/wiki/Allies_of_World_War_II" title="Allies of World War II">Allies of World War II</a> led to the complex and unresolved issues of the <a href="/wiki/Legal_status_of_Taiwan" title="Legal status of Taiwan">legal</a> and <a href="/wiki/Political_status_of_Taiwan" title="Political status of Taiwan">political status of Taiwan</a>.</p>
<p>Taiwan's rapid economic growth in the decades after World War II has transformed it into an <a href="/wiki/Industrialized" title="Industrialized" class="mw-redirect">industrialized</a> <a href="/wiki/Developed_country" title="Developed country">developed country</a> and one of the <a href="/wiki/Four_Asian_Tigers" title="Four Asian Tigers">Four Asian Tigers</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11"><span>[</span>12<span>]</span></a></sup> This economic rise is known as the <a href="/wiki/Taiwan_Miracle" title="Taiwan Miracle">Taiwan Miracle</a>. It is categorized as an advanced economy by the <a href="/wiki/IMF" title="IMF" class="mw-redirect">IMF</a> and as a high-income economy by the <a href="/wiki/World_Bank" title="World Bank">World Bank</a>. Its advanced technology industry plays a key role in the global economy.<sup id="cite_ref-BusinessWeek_Article_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BusinessWeek_Article-12"><span>[</span>13<span>]</span></a></sup> Taiwanese companies manufacture a large portion of the world's consumer electronics, although most of them are now made in their factories in mainland China.<sup id="cite_ref-BusinessWeek_Slideshow_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BusinessWeek_Slideshow-13"><span>[</span>14<span>]</span></a></sup></p>

<h2> <span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span></h2>
<div class="rellink boilerplate further">Further information: <a href="//en.wiktionary.org/wiki/Taiwan#Etymology" class="extiw" title="wikt:Taiwan">Etymology of the word "Taiwan"</a></div>
<p>There are various names of Taiwan in use today, derived from explorers or rulers by each particular period. The former name "Formosa" means "beautiful" in <a href="/wiki/Portuguese_language" title="Portuguese language">Portuguese</a> and was given to Taiwan by the <a href="/wiki/Portuguese_people" title="Portuguese people">Portuguese</a> explorers when they sailed past in the 16th century.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14"><span>[</span>15<span>]</span></a></sup> In the early 17th century, when the <a href="/wiki/Dutch_East_India_Company" title="Dutch East India Company">Dutch East India Company</a> came to build a commercial post at <a href="/wiki/Fort_Zeelandia_(Taiwan)" title="Fort Zeelandia (Taiwan)">Fort Zeelandia</a> (today's <a href="/wiki/Tainan_City" title="Tainan City" class="mw-redirect">Tainan City</a>), they allegedly adopted the name of an aboriginal tribe transliterated as "Tayouan" or "Teyowan" in their records.<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15"><span>[</span>16<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2> <span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span></h2>
<div class="rellink relarticle mainarticle">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/History_of_Taiwan" title="History of Taiwan">History of Taiwan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Timeline_of_Taiwanese_history" title="Timeline of Taiwanese history">Timeline of Taiwanese history</a></div>
<h3> <span class="mw-headline" id="Prehistory_and_early_settlements">Prehistory and early settlements</span></h3>
<div class="rellink relarticle mainarticle">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Prehistory_of_Taiwan" title="Prehistory of Taiwan">Prehistory of Taiwan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Middag" title="Kingdom of Middag">Kingdom of Middag</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Chiefs_Formosa_2.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/Chiefs_Formosa_2.jpg/220px-Chiefs_Formosa_2.jpg" width="220" height="298" class="thumbimage" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Chiefs_Formosa_2.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/skins-1.18/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a></div>
Chiefs belonging to one of the aboriginal tribes of present-day Taiwan.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Evidence of human settlement in Taiwan dates back 30,000 years, although the first inhabitants of Taiwan may have been genetically distinct from any groups currently on the island. About 4,000 years ago, ancestors of current <a href="/wiki/Taiwanese_aborigine" title="Taiwanese aborigine" class="mw-redirect">Taiwanese aborigines</a> settled in Taiwan. These aborigines are genetically related to <a href="/wiki/Austronesian_peoples" title="Austronesian peoples">Austronesian peoples</a>, with a <a href="/wiki/Mitochondria" title="Mitochondria" class="mw-redirect">mitochondrial</a> DNA contribution from a <a href="/wiki/Polynesian" title="Polynesian">Polynesian</a> maternal ancestor, and linguists classify their languages as <a href="/wiki/Austronesian" title="Austronesian" class="mw-redirect">Austronesian</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16"><span>[</span>17<span>]</span></a></sup> It is thought likely that Polynesian ancestry may be traceable throughout Taiwan.</p>
<p>Disputed records from <a href="/wiki/Ancient_China" title="Ancient China" class="mw-redirect">ancient China</a> indicate that the <a href="/wiki/Han_Chinese" title="Han Chinese">Han Chinese</a> might have known of the existence of the main island of Taiwan since the <a href="/wiki/Three_Kingdoms" title="Three Kingdoms">Three Kingdoms</a> period (third century, A.D. 230), having assigned offshore islands in the vicinity names like Greater <a href="/wiki/Liuqiu" title="Liuqiu">Liuqiu</a> and Lesser Liuqiu (<a href="/wiki/Etymology" title="Etymology">etymologically</a>, but perhaps not <a href="/wiki/Semantics" title="Semantics">semantically</a>, identical to <a href="/wiki/Ry%C5%ABky%C5%AB" title="Ryūkyū" class="mw-redirect">Ryūkyū</a> in Japanese), though none of these names has been definitively matched to the main island of Taiwan. <a href="/wiki/Han_Chinese" title="Han Chinese">Han Chinese</a> began settling in the <a href="/wiki/Penghu" title="Penghu">Penghu</a> islands in the 1200s, but Taiwan's hostile tribes and its lack of the trade resources valued in that era rendered it unattractive to all but "occasional adventurers or fishermen engaging in barter" until the 16th century.<sup id="cite_ref-shep_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-shep-17"><span>[</span>18<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Around 1540, Taiwan's main settlement was the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Middag" title="Kingdom of Middag">Kingdom of Middag</a>, a kingdom or supra-tribal alliance located in the central western plains; a monarchy of which the capital was Middag. The city still exists today, and is now known as Dadu. The origins of the name Middag are not documented, but based on its etymology may be attributed to an early Dutch settlement of the island (the name Middag still occurs as a family name in the Netherlands).</p>
<p>During the reign of the <a href="/wiki/Yongzheng_Emperor" title="Yongzheng Emperor">Yongzheng Emperor</a> of Qing later in that century, the population in the traditional Middag territories rose to oppose heavy labor imposed by the Qing authorities, and was brutally quelled by Qing troops and collaborative tribes in 1732, a year after the initial uprising. After this turmoil came to an end, a supra-tribal leadership apparently ceased to exist in the island's central-western plains. In the aftermath of this, the descendants of Middag either fused into the majority "Chinese" population through intermarriage or migrated to present-day Puli, a basin township surrounded by high mountains in central Taiwan.</p>
<h3> <span class="mw-headline" id="European_settlement">European settlement</span></h3>
<div class="rellink relarticle mainarticle">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Dutch_Formosa" title="Dutch Formosa">Dutch Formosa</a> and <a href="/wiki/Spanish_Formosa" title="Spanish Formosa">Spanish Formosa</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:1600_drawing_of_Dutch_ships_in_Taiwan.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/1600_drawing_of_Dutch_ships_in_Taiwan.jpg/300px-1600_drawing_of_Dutch_ships_in_Taiwan.jpg" width="300" height="210" class="thumbimage" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:1600_drawing_of_Dutch_ships_in_Taiwan.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/skins-1.18/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a></div>
1600 drawing of <a href="/wiki/Netherlands" title="Netherlands">Dutch</a> ships in Taiwan.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In 1544, with the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Middag" title="Kingdom of Middag">Kingdom of Middag</a> just being founded, a Portuguese ship sighted the main island of Taiwan and named it <i>Ilha Formosa</i>, which means "Beautiful Island".</p>
<p>In 1624, the <a href="/wiki/Netherlands" title="Netherlands">Dutch</a> established a commercial base on Taiwan and began to import workers from <a href="/wiki/Fujian" title="Fujian">Fujian</a> and <a href="/wiki/Penghu" title="Penghu">Penghu</a> (Pescadores) as laborers, many of whom settled. The Dutch made Taiwan a colony with its colonial capital at Tayoan City (present day <a href="/wiki/Anping,_Tainan" title="Anping, Tainan" class="mw-redirect">Anping, Tainan</a>). Both <i>Tayoan</i> and the island name <i>Taiwan</i> derive from a word in <a href="/wiki/Siraya_language" title="Siraya language">Sirayan</a>, one of the <a href="/wiki/Formosan_languages" title="Formosan languages">Formosan languages</a>.</p>
<p>The Dutch military presence was concentrated at a stronghold called <a href="/wiki/Fort_Zeelandia_(Taiwan)" title="Fort Zeelandia (Taiwan)">Castle Zeelandia</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18"><span>[</span>19<span>]</span></a></sup> The Dutch colonists also started to hunt the native <a href="/wiki/Sika_deer" title="Sika deer" class="mw-redirect">Formosan Sika deer</a> (<i>Cervus nippon taioanus</i>) that inhabited Taiwan, contributing to the eventual extinction of the subspecies on the island.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19"><span>[</span>20<span>]</span></a></sup> However, the subspecies was kept alive in captivity and subsequent reintroduction of the subspecies into the wild has been successful.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20"><span>[</span>21<span>]</span></a></sup> Furthermore, this contributed to the subsequent identification of native tribes.</p>
<p>In 1626, the Spanish landed on and occupied northern Taiwan (Keelong and Tanshui) as a base to extend its commercial trading. This colonial period lasted 16 years until 1642.</p>
<h3> <span class="mw-headline" id="Kingdom_of_Tungning">Kingdom of Tungning</span></h3>
<div class="rellink relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Tungning" title="Kingdom of Tungning">Kingdom of Tungning</a></div>
<p>Chinese naval and troop forces of Southern Fujian defeated the Dutch in 1662, subsequently expelling the <a href="/wiki/Dutch_Empire" title="Dutch Empire">Dutch government</a> and military from the island. They were led by <a href="/wiki/Koxinga" title="Koxinga">Koxinga</a> (Zheng Chenggong). Following the fall of the <a href="/wiki/Ming_Dynasty" title="Ming Dynasty">Ming Dynasty</a>, Koxinga retreated to Taiwan as a self-styled Ming loyalist and established the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Tungning" title="Kingdom of Tungning">Kingdom of Tungning</a> (1662–1683). Koxinga established his capital at <a href="/wiki/Tainan_City" title="Tainan City" class="mw-redirect">Tainan</a> and he and his heirs, <a href="/wiki/Zheng_Jing" title="Zheng Jing">Zheng Jing</a>, who ruled from 1662 to 1682, and <a href="/wiki/Zheng_Keshuang" title="Zheng Keshuang">Zheng Keshuang</a>, who served less than a year, continued to launch raids on the south-east coast of mainland China well into the <a href="/wiki/Qing_Dynasty" title="Qing Dynasty">Qing Dynasty</a>, attempting to recapture mainland China.</p>
<h3> <span class="mw-headline" id="Qing_Dynasty_rule">Qing Dynasty rule</span></h3>
<div class="rellink relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Taiwan_under_Qing_Dynasty_rule" title="Taiwan under Qing Dynasty rule">Taiwan under Qing Dynasty rule</a></div>
<p>In 1683, following the defeat of Koxinga's grandson by an armada led by Admiral <a href="/wiki/Shi_Lang" title="Shi Lang">Shi Lang</a> of Southern Fujian, the <a href="/wiki/Qing" title="Qing" class="mw-redirect">Qing</a> formally annexed Taiwan, placing it under the jurisdiction of Fujian province. The Qing imperial government tried to reduce piracy and vagrancy in the area, issuing a series of edicts to manage immigration and respect aboriginal land rights. Immigrants mostly from Southern Fujian continued to enter Taiwan. The border between taxpaying lands and "savage" lands shifted eastward, with some aborigines '<a href="/wiki/Sinicization" title="Sinicization">Sinicizing</a>' while others retreated into the mountains. During this time, there were a number of conflicts between <a href="/wiki/Han_Chinese" title="Han Chinese">Chinese</a> from different regions of Southern Fujian, and between Southern Fujian Chinese and aborigines.</p>
<p>Northern Taiwan and the Penghu Islands were the scene of an important subsidiary campaign in the <a href="/wiki/Sino-French_War" title="Sino-French War" class="mw-redirect">Sino-French War</a> (August 1884 to April 1885). The French occupied Keelung from 1 October 1884 to 22 June 1885 and the Penghu Islands from 31 March to 22 July 1885. A French attempt to capture Tamsui was defeated at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Tamsui" title="Battle of Tamsui">Battle of Tamsui</a> (8 October 1884). Several battles were fought around Keelung between October 1884 and March 1885 between Liu Ming-ch'uan's Army of Northern Taiwan and Colonel <a href="/wiki/Jacques_Duchesne" title="Jacques Duchesne">Jacques Duchesne</a>'s Formosa Expeditionary Corps. The <a href="/wiki/Keelung_Campaign" title="Keelung Campaign">Keelung Campaign</a>, despite some notable French tactical victories, ended in a stalemate. The <a href="/wiki/Pescadores_Campaign" title="Pescadores Campaign" class="mw-redirect">Pescadores Campaign</a> was a French victory, but had no long-term consequences. The French evacuated both Keelung and the Penghu archipelago at the end of the war.</p>
<p>In 1885, the Qing upgraded Taiwan's status from <a href="/wiki/Prefecture#The_ancient_sense" title="Prefecture">prefecture</a> of <a href="/wiki/Fujian" title="Fujian">Fujian</a> to full <a href="/wiki/Province_of_China" title="Province of China" class="mw-redirect">province</a>, the twentieth in the empire, with its capital at <a href="/wiki/Taipei" title="Taipei">Taipei</a>. This was accompanied by a modernization drive that included building Taiwan's first railroad and starting a postal service.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21"><span>[</span>22<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3> <span class="mw-headline" id="Empire_of_Japan_rule">Empire of Japan rule</span></h3>
<div class="rellink relarticle mainarticle">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Taiwan_under_Japanese_rule" title="Taiwan under Japanese rule">Taiwan under Japanese rule</a> and <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_Formosa" title="Republic of Formosa">Republic of Formosa</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:TaipeiROCPresidentialOfficeBuilding.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/TaipeiROCPresidentialOfficeBuilding.jpg/220px-TaipeiROCPresidentialOfficeBuilding.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:TaipeiROCPresidentialOfficeBuilding.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/skins-1.18/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a></div>
The building currently known as the ROC <a href="/wiki/Presidential_Building_(Taipei)" title="Presidential Building (Taipei)" class="mw-redirect">Presidential Office</a> was originally built as the Office of the <a href="/wiki/Governor-General_of_Taiwan" title="Governor-General of Taiwan">Governor-General</a> by the Japanese government.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Japan had sought to control Taiwan since 1592, when <a href="/wiki/Toyotomi_Hideyoshi" title="Toyotomi Hideyoshi">Toyotomi Hideyoshi</a> began extending Japanese influence overseas. In 1609, the <a href="/wiki/Tokugawa_Shogunate" title="Tokugawa Shogunate" class="mw-redirect">Tokugawa Shogunate</a> sent <a href="/wiki/Arima_Harunobu" title="Arima Harunobu">Arima Harunobu</a> on an exploratory mission. In 1616, Murayama Toan led an unsuccessful invasion of the island.</p>
<p>In 1871, an <a href="/wiki/Mudan_Incident_of_1871" title="Mudan Incident of 1871">Okinawan vessel shipwrecked</a> on the southern tip of Taiwan and the crew of fifty-four was beheaded by the <a href="/wiki/Paiwan_people" title="Paiwan people">Paiwan</a> aborigines. The <a href="/wiki/Ry%C5%ABky%C5%AB_Kingdom" title="Ryūkyū Kingdom">Ryūkyū Kingdom</a> kept a tributary relationship with Great Qing Empire at the same time was subordinate to <a href="/wiki/Satsuma_Domain" title="Satsuma Domain">Satsuma Domain</a> of Japan. When Japan sought compensation from <a href="/wiki/Great_Qing" title="Great Qing" class="mw-redirect">Qing China</a>, it was first rejected because Qing considered the incident an internal affair since Taiwan was a prefecture of Fujian Province of Qing and the Ryūkyū Kingdom was a tributary of Qing. When Japanese foreign minister <a href="/wiki/Soejima_Taneomi" title="Soejima Taneomi">Soejima Taneomi</a> asked the compensation again claiming four of the victims were Japanese citizens from <a href="/wiki/Okayama" title="Okayama">Okayama</a> prefecture of Japan, Qing officials rejected the demand on the grounds that the "wild" and "unsubjugated" aboriginals (<a href="/wiki/Traditional_Chinese_characters" title="Traditional Chinese characters">traditional Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh-Hant" xml:lang="zh-Hant">台灣生番</span>; <a href="/wiki/Simplified_Chinese_characters" title="Simplified Chinese characters">simplified Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh-Hans" xml:lang="zh-Hans">台湾生番</span>; <a href="/wiki/Pinyin" title="Pinyin">pinyin</a>: <em>Táiwān shēngfān</em>) were outside its jurisdiction. Such aboriginals were treated extremely harshly; American consul J.W. Davidson described how the Chinese in Taiwan ate and traded in their aboriginal victims' flesh.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22"><span>[</span>23<span>]</span></a></sup> The open renunciation of sovereignty led to a Japanese invasion of Taiwan. In 1874, an <a href="/wiki/Taiwan_Expedition_of_1874" title="Taiwan Expedition of 1874">expeditionary force</a> of three thousand troops was sent to the island. There were about thirty Taiwanese and 543 Japanese casualties (twelve in battle and 531 by <a href="/wiki/Endemic_(epidemiology)" title="Endemic (epidemiology)">endemic</a> diseases for the Japanese side).<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23"><span>[</span>24<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24"><span>[</span>25<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25"><span>[</span>26<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26"><span>[</span>27<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Japanese_Soldier_Entering_Taipei(1895).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Japanese_Soldier_Entering_Taipei%281895%29.jpg/170px-Japanese_Soldier_Entering_Taipei%281895%29.jpg" width="170" height="207" class="thumbimage" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Japanese_Soldier_Entering_Taipei(1895).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/skins-1.18/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Japanese_people" title="Japanese people">Japanese</a> Soldiers Entering <a href="/wiki/Taipei" title="Taipei">Taipei</a> City in 1895 after the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Shimonoseki" title="Treaty of Shimonoseki">Treaty of Shimonoseki</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The Qing Dynasty was defeated in the <a href="/wiki/First_Sino-Japanese_War" title="First Sino-Japanese War">First Sino-Japanese War</a> (1894–1895) and Taiwan and Penghu were <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Shimonoseki" title="Treaty of Shimonoseki">ceded in full sovereignty</a> to the <a href="/wiki/Empire_of_Japan" title="Empire of Japan">Empire of Japan</a>. Inhabitants wishing to remain Qing subjects were given a two-year grace period to sell their property and move to mainland China. Very few Taiwanese saw this as feasible.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27"><span>[</span>28<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>On 25 May 1895, a group of pro-Qing high officials proclaimed the <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_Formosa" title="Republic of Formosa">Republic of Formosa</a> to resist impending Japanese rule. Japanese forces entered the capital at Tainan and quelled this resistance on 21 October 1895.<sup class="Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources from August 2009">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>The Japanese rulers were instrumental in the industrialization of the island; they extended the railroads and other transportation networks, built an extensive sanitation system and revised the public school system. During this period, both rice and sugarcane production greatly increased. By 1939, Taiwan was the seventh greatest sugar producer in the world.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28"><span>[</span>29<span>]</span></a></sup> Still, the Taiwanese and Aborigines were classified as second- and third-class citizens. Large-scale violence continued in the first decade of rule. Japan launched over 160 battles to destroy Taiwan's aboriginal tribes during its 51-year rule of the island ...'<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29"><span>[</span>30<span>]</span></a></sup> Around 1935, the Japanese began an island-wide <a href="/wiki/Japanization" title="Japanization">assimilation</a> project to bind the island more firmly to the Japanese Empire and people were taught to see themselves as Japanese. During World War II, tens of thousands of Taiwanese served in the Japanese military.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30"><span>[</span>31<span>]</span></a></sup> For example, former ROC President <a href="/wiki/Lee_Teng-hui" title="Lee Teng-hui">Lee Teng-hui</a>'s elder brother served in the Japanese navy and died while on duty in the Philippines in February 1945.</p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Imperial_Japanese_Navy" title="Imperial Japanese Navy">Imperial Japanese Navy</a> operated heavily out of Taiwan. The "<a href="/wiki/The_Japanese_Navy_Taiwan_and_South_Pacific_Mandate_political_project" title="The Japanese Navy Taiwan and South Pacific Mandate political project" class="mw-redirect">South Strike Group</a>" was based out of the <a href="/wiki/Taihoku_Imperial_University" title="Taihoku Imperial University" class="mw-redirect">Taihoku Imperial University</a> in Taiwan. Many of the Japanese forces participating in the <a href="/wiki/Aerial_Battle_of_Taiwan-Okinawa" title="Aerial Battle of Taiwan-Okinawa" class="mw-redirect">Aerial Battle of Taiwan-Okinawa</a> were based in Taiwan. Important Japanese military bases and industrial centers throughout Taiwan, like Kaohsiung, were targets of heavy <a href="/wiki/Raid_on_Taipei" title="Raid on Taipei">American bombing</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31"><span>[</span>32<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Taiwan under Empire of Japan rule ended after it lost World War II and signed the <a href="/wiki/Instrument_of_Surrender_of_Japan" title="Instrument of Surrender of Japan" class="mw-redirect">Instrument of Surrender of Japan</a> on 14 August 1945. But the Japanese rule had long lasting effects on Taiwan. Education became compulsory for school age children. Significant parts of Taiwanese infrastructure were started under the Japanese rule. The current <a href="/wiki/Presidential_Office_Building,_Taipei" title="Presidential Office Building, Taipei">Presidential Building</a> was also built during that time. In 1938 there were 309,000 <a href="/wiki/Japanese_diaspora" title="Japanese diaspora">Japanese settlers</a> in Taiwan.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32"><span>[</span>33<span>]</span></a></sup> After World War II, most of the Japanese <a href="/wiki/World_War_II_evacuation_and_expulsion" title="World War II evacuation and expulsion">were repatriated to Japan</a>.</p>
<h3> <span class="mw-headline" id="Republic_of_China_rule">Republic of China rule</span></h3>
<div class="rellink relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Taiwan_after_World_War_II" title="Taiwan after World War II">Taiwan after World War II</a></div>
<h4> <span class="mw-headline" id="Kuomintang_Martial_Law_Era">Kuomintang Martial Law Era</span></h4>
<div class="rellink relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/White_Terror_(Taiwan)" title="White Terror (Taiwan)">White Terror (Taiwan)</a></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Cairo_Conference" title="Cairo Conference">Cairo Conference</a> from 22 to 26 November 1943 in <a href="/wiki/Cairo,_Egypt" title="Cairo, Egypt" class="mw-redirect">Cairo, Egypt</a> was held to address the <a href="/wiki/Allies_of_World_War_II" title="Allies of World War II">Allied</a> position against Japan during World War II, and to make decisions about postwar Asia. One of the three main clauses of the <a href="/wiki/Cairo_Declaration" title="Cairo Declaration">Cairo Declaration</a> was that "all the territories Japan has stolen from China, including <a href="/wiki/Manchuria" title="Manchuria">Manchuria</a>, Taiwan and the <a href="/wiki/Pescadores" title="Pescadores" class="mw-redirect">Pescadores</a>, shall be restored to the Republic of China'. However, many challenged that the document was merely a statement of intent for possible reference used for those who would draft the post-war peace treaty and that it was a press release without force of law to transfer sovereignty from Taiwan to the Republic of China. The general counter-argument for this claim is that while the Cairo Declaration itself was a non-binding declaration, it was given legal effect by the Japanese Instrument of Surrender.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33"><span>[</span>34<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>On 25 October 1945, the <a href="/wiki/US_Navy" title="US Navy" class="mw-redirect">US Navy</a> ferried ROC troops to Taiwan in order to accept the formal surrender of Japanese military forces in <a href="/wiki/Taipei" title="Taipei">Taipei</a> (then called <a href="/wiki/Taihoku" title="Taihoku" class="mw-redirect">Taihoku</a>). General <a href="/wiki/Rikichi_And%C5%8D" title="Rikichi Andō">Rikichi Andō</a>, governor-general of Taiwan and commander-in-chief of all Japanese forces on the island, signed the <a href="/wiki/Japanese_Instrument_of_Surrender" title="Japanese Instrument of Surrender">instrument of surrender</a> and handed it over to General <a href="/wiki/Chen_Yi_(Kuomintang)" title="Chen Yi (Kuomintang)">Chen Yi</a> of the ROC military to complete the official turnover. Chen Yi proclaimed that day to be "<a href="/wiki/Retrocession_Day" title="Retrocession Day">Retrocession Day of Taiwan</a>," a proclamation which was not recognized by the Allies.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34"><span>[</span>35<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35"><span>[</span>36<span>]</span></a></sup> The ROC administration of Taiwan under <a href="/wiki/Chen_Yi_(KMT)" title="Chen Yi (KMT)" class="mw-redirect">Chen Yi</a> was strained by social and political instabilities, which were compounded by economic woes, such as <a href="/wiki/Hyperinflation" title="Hyperinflation">hyperinflation</a>. Furthermore, cultural and linguistic conflicts between Taiwanese and the mainland Chinese quickly led to the loss of popular support for the new government.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36"><span>[</span>37<span>]</span></a></sup> This culminated in a series of severe clashes between the ROC occupiers and the Taiwanese, in turn leading to the <a href="/wiki/228_incident" title="228 incident" class="mw-redirect">228 incident</a> (an estimated 20,000-30,000 civilians were executed by the ROC Army) and the reign of <a href="/wiki/White_Terror_(Taiwan)" title="White Terror (Taiwan)">White Terror</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37"><span>[</span>38<span>]</span></a></sup> During the White Terror, a period of the longest martial law in the world, over 38 years, was imposed and many, many thousands of Taiwanese were arrested, tortured, imprisoned and executed for their real or perceived opposition to the Kuomintang Party. Since these people were mainly from the intellectual and social elite an entire generation of political and social leaders was decimated. It was not until 2008 that a public apology was made for those actions. No form of restitution or compensation has ever been made (as of 2010).<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38"><span>[</span>39<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:277px;"><a href="/wiki/File:%E4%B8%AD%E8%8F%AF%E6%B0%91%E5%9C%8B%E7%AC%AC%E4%B8%80%E4%BD%8D%E6%B0%91%E9%81%B8%E9%A6%96%E9%83%BD%E5%B8%82%E9%95%B7%E5%90%B3%E4%B8%89%E9%80%A3%E6%96%BC1951%E5%B9%B4%E5%8B%9D%E9%81%B8%E5%BE%8C.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4f/%E4%B8%AD%E8%8F%AF%E6%B0%91%E5%9C%8B%E7%AC%AC%E4%B8%80%E4%BD%8D%E6%B0%91%E9%81%B8%E9%A6%96%E9%83%BD%E5%B8%82%E9%95%B7%E5%90%B3%E4%B8%89%E9%80%A3%E6%96%BC1951%E5%B9%B4%E5%8B%9D%E9%81%B8%E5%BE%8C.jpg/275px-%E4%B8%AD%E8%8F%AF%E6%B0%91%E5%9C%8B%E7%AC%AC%E4%B8%80%E4%BD%8D%E6%B0%91%E9%81%B8%E9%A6%96%E9%83%BD%E5%B8%82%E9%95%B7%E5%90%B3%E4%B8%89%E9%80%A3%E6%96%BC1951%E5%B9%B4%E5%8B%9D%E9%81%B8%E5%BE%8C.jpg" width="275" height="217" class="thumbimage" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:%E4%B8%AD%E8%8F%AF%E6%B0%91%E5%9C%8B%E7%AC%AC%E4%B8%80%E4%BD%8D%E6%B0%91%E9%81%B8%E9%A6%96%E9%83%BD%E5%B8%82%E9%95%B7%E5%90%B3%E4%B8%89%E9%80%A3%E6%96%BC1951%E5%B9%B4%E5%8B%9D%E9%81%B8%E5%BE%8C.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/skins-1.18/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a></div>
Non-Kuomintang Taiwanese politician Wu San-lian (2L) celebrated his landslide victory (65.5%) in the first-time <a href="/wiki/Taipei_city" title="Taipei city" class="mw-redirect">Taipei city</a> mayoral election in January <a href="/wiki/1951" title="1951">1951</a> with his supporters. <a href="/wiki/Taipei" title="Taipei">Taipei</a> is the capital of the <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_China" title="Republic of China">Republic of China</a> (Taiwan) since December <a href="/wiki/1949" title="1949">1949</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In 1949, during the <a href="/wiki/Chinese_Civil_War" title="Chinese Civil War">Chinese Civil War</a>, the ROC government, led by President <a href="/wiki/Chiang_Kai-shek" title="Chiang Kai-shek">Chiang Kai-shek</a>, retreated from <a href="/wiki/Nanjing" title="Nanjing">Nanjing</a> (then romanised as "Nanking") to <a href="/wiki/Taipei" title="Taipei">Taipei</a>, Taiwan's largest city. The ROC continued to claim sovereignty over all "China", which the ROC defines to include <a href="/wiki/Mainland_China" title="Mainland China">mainland China</a>, Taiwan, <a href="/wiki/Outer_Mongolia" title="Outer Mongolia">Outer Mongolia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Administrative_divisions_of_the_Republic_of_China" title="Administrative divisions of the Republic of China">other areas</a>. The only remaining portions of territory besides Taiwan under ROC control are the <a href="/wiki/Kinmen" title="Kinmen">Kinmen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Matsu_Islands" title="Matsu Islands">Matsu Islands</a>, and two major islands of <a href="/wiki/Dongsha_Islands" title="Dongsha Islands" class="mw-redirect">Dongsha Islands</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nansha_Islands" title="Nansha Islands" class="mw-redirect">Nansha Islands</a>. In mainland China, the victorious Communists established the PRC, claiming to be the sole and only China (which they claimed included Taiwan) and the ROC no longer existed.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39"><span>[</span>40<span>]</span></a></sup> However, since PRC's establishment, it never controlled any of the territories the ROC government currently governs.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:277px;"><a href="/wiki/File:%E7%BE%8E%E5%9C%8B%E7%B8%BD%E7%B5%B1%E8%89%BE%E6%A3%AE%E8%B1%AA%E6%96%BC1960%E5%B9%B46%E6%9C%88%E8%A8%AA%E5%95%8F%E8%87%BA%E7%81%A3%E5%8F%B0%E5%8C%97%E6%99%82%E8%88%87%E8%94%A3%E4%B8%AD%E6%AD%A3%E7%B8%BD%E7%B5%B1-2.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/%E7%BE%8E%E5%9C%8B%E7%B8%BD%E7%B5%B1%E8%89%BE%E6%A3%AE%E8%B1%AA%E6%96%BC1960%E5%B9%B46%E6%9C%88%E8%A8%AA%E5%95%8F%E8%87%BA%E7%81%A3%E5%8F%B0%E5%8C%97%E6%99%82%E8%88%87%E8%94%A3%E4%B8%AD%E6%AD%A3%E7%B8%BD%E7%B5%B1-2.jpg/275px-%E7%BE%8E%E5%9C%8B%E7%B8%BD%E7%B5%B1%E8%89%BE%E6%A3%AE%E8%B1%AA%E6%96%BC1960%E5%B9%B46%E6%9C%88%E8%A8%AA%E5%95%8F%E8%87%BA%E7%81%A3%E5%8F%B0%E5%8C%97%E6%99%82%E8%88%87%E8%94%A3%E4%B8%AD%E6%AD%A3%E7%B8%BD%E7%B5%B1-2.jpg" width="275" height="174" class="thumbimage" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:%E7%BE%8E%E5%9C%8B%E7%B8%BD%E7%B5%B1%E8%89%BE%E6%A3%AE%E8%B1%AA%E6%96%BC1960%E5%B9%B46%E6%9C%88%E8%A8%AA%E5%95%8F%E8%87%BA%E7%81%A3%E5%8F%B0%E5%8C%97%E6%99%82%E8%88%87%E8%94%A3%E4%B8%AD%E6%AD%A3%E7%B8%BD%E7%B5%B1-2.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/skins-1.18/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a></div>
With President <a href="/wiki/Chiang_Kai-shek" title="Chiang Kai-shek">Chiang Kai-shek</a>, the U.S. President <a href="/wiki/Dwight_D._Eisenhower" title="Dwight D. Eisenhower">Dwight D. Eisenhower</a> waved hands to Taiwanese people during his visit to <a href="/wiki/Taipei" title="Taipei">Taipei</a>, Taiwan in June 1960.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Some 2 million people, consisting mainly of soldiers, <a href="/wiki/Kuomintang" title="Kuomintang">Kuomintang</a> party (KMT, Chinese Nationalist Party) members and most importantly the intellectual and business elites, were evacuated from mainland China and arrived in Taiwan around that time. In addition, as part of its escape from Communists in mainland China, the ROC government relocated to Taipei with many national treasures including gold reserves and foreign currency reserves.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40"><span>[</span>41<span>]</span></a></sup> From this period through the 1980s, Taiwan was governed in a state of Martial Law. Little to no distinction was made between the government and the Nationalist party, with public property, government property, and party property being largely interchangeable. Government workers and party members were mostly indistinguishable, with many government workers required to become KMT members, and party workers paid salaries and promised retirement benefits along the lines of government employees. In addition, the creation of other parties was outlawed, and many political opponents were persecuted and incarcerated.<sup class="Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources from May 2008">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>The ROC remained a <i>de facto</i> <a href="/wiki/One-party_state" title="One-party state" class="mw-redirect">one-party state</a> under <a href="/wiki/Martial_law" title="Martial law">martial law</a> under the "<a href="/wiki/Temporary_Provisions_Effective_During_the_Period_of_Communist_Rebellion" title="Temporary Provisions Effective During the Period of Communist Rebellion">Temporary Provisions Effective During the Period of Communist Rebellion</a>", from 1948 to 1987, when the ROC Presidents <a href="/wiki/Chiang_Ching-kuo" title="Chiang Ching-kuo">Chiang Ching-kuo</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lee_Teng-hui" title="Lee Teng-hui">Lee Teng-hui</a> gradually <a href="/wiki/Liberalism" title="Liberalism">liberalized</a> and democratized the system. With the advent of democratization, the issue of the <a href="/wiki/Political_status_of_Taiwan" title="Political status of Taiwan">political status of Taiwan</a> has resurfaced as a controversial issue (previously, discussion of anything other than unification under the ROC was <a href="/wiki/Taboo" title="Taboo">taboo</a>).</p>
<p>As the Chinese Civil War continued without truce, the ROC built up military fortifications throughout Taiwan. Within this effort, former KMT soldiers built the now famous <a href="/wiki/Central_Cross-Island_Highway" title="Central Cross-Island Highway">Central Cross-Island Highway</a> through the <a href="/wiki/Taroko_Gorge" title="Taroko Gorge" class="mw-redirect">Taroko Gorge</a> in the 1950s. The two sides would continue to engage in sporadic military clashes with seldom publicized details well into the 1960s on the nearby islands with an unknown number of <a href="/wiki/Project_National_Glory" title="Project National Glory">night raids</a>. During the <a href="/wiki/Second_Taiwan_Strait_Crisis" title="Second Taiwan Strait Crisis">Second Taiwan Strait Crisis</a> in September 1958, Taiwan's landscape saw <a href="/wiki/Nike-Hercules_missile" title="Nike-Hercules missile" class="mw-redirect">Nike-Hercules missile</a> batteries added, with the formation of the 1st Missile Battalion Chinese Army that would not be deactivated until 1997. Newer generations of missile batteries have since replaced the Nike Hercules systems throughout the island.</p>
<p>During the 1960s and 1970s, Taiwan began to develop into a prosperous, industrialized <a href="/wiki/Developed_country" title="Developed country">developed country</a> with a strong and dynamic economy, becoming one of the <a href="/wiki/Four_Asian_Tigers" title="Four Asian Tigers">Four Asian Tigers</a> while maintaining martial law and under the KMT monopoly. Because of the <a href="/wiki/Cold_War" title="Cold War">Cold War</a>, most Western nations and the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations" title="United Nations">United Nations</a> regarded the ROC as the sole legitimate government of China until the 1970s, when most nations began switching recognition to the PRC (see <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_General_Assembly_Resolution_2758" title="United Nations General Assembly Resolution 2758">United Nations General Assembly Resolution 2758</a>).</p>
<h4> <span class="mw-headline" id="Post-Martial_Law">Post-Martial Law</span></h4>
<div class="rellink relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_Taiwan#Democratic_reforms" title="History of Taiwan">History of Taiwan#Democratic reforms</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Chiang_Kai-shek" title="Chiang Kai-shek">Chiang Kai-shek</a>'s eventual successor, his son <a href="/wiki/Chiang_Ching-kuo" title="Chiang Ching-kuo">Chiang Ching-kuo</a>, began to liberalize the ROC's political system in mid-1980s. In 1984, the younger Chiang selected <a href="/wiki/Lee_Teng-hui" title="Lee Teng-hui">Lee Teng-hui</a>, an ethnically Taiwanese and U.S.-educated technocrat, to be his vice president. In 1986, the <a href="/wiki/Democratic_Progressive_Party" title="Democratic Progressive Party">Democratic Progressive Party</a> (DPP) was formed and inaugurated as the first opposition party in Taiwan to counter the KMT. A year later Chiang Ching-kuo lifted martial law.</p>
<p>After the death of Chiang Ching-Kuo in January 1988, Dr. Lee Teng-hui succeed as President and became the first ethnically Taiwanese president of the ROC. Lee continued to democratize the government and decrease the concentration of government authority in the hands of mainland Chinese. Under Lee, Taiwan underwent a process of <a href="/wiki/Taiwanese_localization_movement" title="Taiwanese localization movement" class="mw-redirect">localization</a> in which Taiwanese culture and history were promoted over a pan-China viewpoint in contrast to earlier KMT policies which had promoted a Chinese identity. Lee's reforms included printing banknotes from the Central Bank rather than the Provincial Bank of Taiwan, and streamlining the <a href="/wiki/Taiwan_Province" title="Taiwan Province">Taiwan Provincial Government</a> with most of its functions transferred to the <a href="/wiki/Executive_Yuan" title="Executive Yuan">Executive Yuan</a>. Under Lee, the original members of the <a href="/wiki/Legislative_Yuan" title="Legislative Yuan">Legislative Yuan</a> and <a href="/wiki/National_Assembly_of_the_Republic_of_China" title="National Assembly of the Republic of China">National Assembly</a>, elected in 1947 to represent mainland Chinese constituencies and having taken the seats without re-election for more than four decades, were forced to resign in 1991. The previously nominal representation in the Legislative Yuan was brought to an end, to reflect the reality that the ROC government had no jurisdiction over mainland China, and vice versa. Restrictions on the use of <a href="/wiki/Taiwanese_Hokkien" title="Taiwanese Hokkien">Taiwanese Hokkien</a> in the broadcast media and in schools were lifted as well. During later years of Lee's administration, he was involved in corruption controversies relating to government release of land and weapons purchase, although no legal proceedings commenced.</p>
<p>In the 1990s, the ROC continued its democratic reforms, as President <a href="/wiki/Lee_Teng-hui" title="Lee Teng-hui">Lee Teng-hui</a> was elected by the first popular vote held in the ROC during the 1996 Presidential election. In 2000, <a href="/wiki/Chen_Shui-bian" title="Chen Shui-bian">Chen Shui-bian</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Democratic_Progressive_Party" title="Democratic Progressive Party">DPP</a>, was elected as the first non-<a href="/wiki/Kuomintang" title="Kuomintang">KMT</a> President and was re-elected to serve his second and last term since 2004. Polarized politics has emerged in Taiwan with the formation of the <a href="/wiki/Pan-Blue_Coalition" title="Pan-Blue Coalition">Pan-Blue Coalition</a> of parties led by the KMT, favoring eventual <a href="/wiki/Chinese_reunification" title="Chinese reunification">Chinese reunification</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Pan-Green_Coalition" title="Pan-Green Coalition">Pan-Green Coalition</a> of parties led by the <a href="/wiki/Democratic_Progressive_Party" title="Democratic Progressive Party">DPP</a>, favoring an eventual and official declaration of <a href="/wiki/Taiwan_independence" title="Taiwan independence">Taiwan independence</a>.</p>
<p>On 30 September 2007, the ruling <a href="/wiki/Democratic_Progressive_Party" title="Democratic Progressive Party">Democratic Progressive Party</a> approved a <a href="/wiki/Resolution_(law)" title="Resolution (law)">resolution</a> asserting separate identity from China and called for the enactment of a new <a href="/wiki/Constitution" title="Constitution">constitution</a> for a "<i>normal country</i>". It also called for general use of "<i>Taiwan</i>" as the island's name, without abolishing its formal name, the <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_China" title="Republic of China">Republic of China</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41"><span>[</span>42<span>]</span></a></sup> The Chen administration also pushed for referendums on national defense and UN entry in the 2004 and 2008 elections, which failed due to voter turnout below the required legal threshold of 50% of all registered voters.<sup id="cite_ref-lam200803_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-lam200803-42"><span>[</span>43<span>]</span></a></sup> The Chen administration was dogged by public concerns over reduced economic growth, legislative gridlock due to a pan-blue, opposition controlled Legislative Yuan, and corruption involving the First Family as well as government officials.<sup id="cite_ref-economist20080323_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-economist20080323-43"><span>[</span>44<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ft20080325_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ft20080325-44"><span>[</span>45<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The KMT increased its majority in the Legislative Yuan in the <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_China_legislative_election,_2008" title="Republic of China legislative election, 2008">January 2008 legislative elections</a>, while its nominee <a href="/wiki/Ma_Ying-jeou" title="Ma Ying-jeou">Ma Ying-jeou</a> went on to <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_China_presidential_election,_2008" title="Republic of China presidential election, 2008">win the presidency</a> in March of the same year, campaigning on a platform of increased economic growth, and better ties with the PRC under a policy of "<a href="/wiki/Special_non-state-to-state_relations" title="Special non-state-to-state relations">mutual nondenial</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-lam200803_42-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-lam200803-42"><span>[</span>43<span>]</span></a></sup> Ma took office on 20 May 2008. Part of the rationale for campaigning for closer economic ties with the PRC stem from the strong economic growth China attained since joining the <a href="/wiki/World_Trade_Organization" title="World Trade Organization">World Trade Organization</a>. However, some analysts say that despite the election of Ma Ying-jeou, the diplomatic and military tensions with the PRC have not been reduced.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45"><span>[</span>46<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2> <span class="mw-headline" id="Geography">Geography</span></h2>
<div class="rellink relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Geography_of_Taiwan" title="Geography of Taiwan">Geography of Taiwan</a></div>
<div class="rellink boilerplate seealso">See also: <a href="/wiki/Administrative_divisions_of_the_Republic_of_China" title="Administrative divisions of the Republic of China">Administrative divisions of the Republic of China</a>&#160;and <a href="/wiki/List_of_islands_of_the_Republic_of_China#Taiwan" title="List of islands of the Republic of China">List of islands of the Republic of China#Taiwan</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:TAIWAN_Karte_Gross.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/TAIWAN_Karte_Gross.jpg/220px-TAIWAN_Karte_Gross.jpg" width="220" height="272" class="thumbimage" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:TAIWAN_Karte_Gross.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/skins-1.18/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a></div>
Map of Taiwan</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:San_Guang_River,_Fusing,_Taoyuan,_Taiwan_-_20080601.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/San_Guang_River%2C_Fusing%2C_Taoyuan%2C_Taiwan_-_20080601.jpg/220px-San_Guang_River%2C_Fusing%2C_Taoyuan%2C_Taiwan_-_20080601.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:San_Guang_River,_Fusing,_Taoyuan,_Taiwan_-_20080601.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/skins-1.18/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a></div>
Landscape of Taiwan.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The island of Taiwan lies some 180 kilometers off the southeastern coast of China, across the <a href="/wiki/Taiwan_Strait" title="Taiwan Strait">Taiwan Strait</a>, and has an area of 35,881&#160;km<sup>2</sup> (13,853.7&#160;sq&#160;mi). If included the <a href="/wiki/Penghu_Islands" title="Penghu Islands" class="mw-redirect">Penghu Islands</a> which is now a nominal county under the Taiwan Province, administered by the <a href="/wiki/Executive_Yuan" title="Executive Yuan">Executive Yuan</a>, Taiwan's area is 36,008&#160;km<sup>2</sup> (13,902.8&#160;sq&#160;mi).<sup id="cite_ref-TWAP_0-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TWAP-0"><span>[</span>1<span>]</span></a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/East_China_Sea" title="East China Sea">East China Sea</a> lies to the north, the <a href="/wiki/Philippine_Sea" title="Philippine Sea">Philippine Sea</a> to the east, the <a href="/wiki/Luzon_Strait" title="Luzon Strait">Luzon Strait</a> directly to the south and the <a href="/wiki/South_China_Sea" title="South China Sea">South China Sea</a> to the southwest. The island is characterized by the contrast between the eastern two-thirds, consisting mostly of rugged mountains running in five ranges from the northern to the southern tip of the island, and the flat to gently rolling <a href="/wiki/Chianan_Plain" title="Chianan Plain">Chianan Plains</a> in the west that are also home to most of Taiwan's population. Taiwan's highest point is <a href="/wiki/Yu_Shan" title="Yu Shan" class="mw-redirect">Yu Shan</a> at 3,952 meters, and there are five other peaks over 3,500 meters. This makes it the world's <a href="/wiki/List_of_islands_by_highest_point" title="List of islands by highest point">fourth-highest island</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46"><span>[</span>47<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Taroko_National_Park" title="Taroko National Park">Taroko National Park</a>, located on the mountainous eastern side of the island, has good examples of mountainous terrain, gorges and <a href="/wiki/Erosion" title="Erosion">erosion</a> caused by a swiftly flowing river.</p>
<p>The shape of the main island of Taiwan is similar to a <a href="/wiki/Sweet_potato" title="Sweet potato">sweet potato</a> seen in a south-to-north direction, and therefore, Taiwanese, especially the <a href="/wiki/Min-nan" title="Min-nan" class="mw-redirect">Min-nan</a> division, often call themselves "children of the Sweet Potato."<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47"><span>[</span>48<span>]</span></a></sup> There are also other interpretations of the island shape, one of which is a <a href="/wiki/Whale" title="Whale">whale</a> in the ocean (the Pacific Ocean) if viewed in a west-to-east direction, which is a common orientation in ancient maps, plotted either by <a href="/wiki/Western_world" title="Western world">Western</a> explorers or the <a href="/wiki/Great_Qing" title="Great Qing" class="mw-redirect">Great Qing</a>.</p>
<h3> <span class="mw-headline" id="Geology">Geology</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:DabajianMountain.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/DabajianMountain.jpg/220px-DabajianMountain.jpg" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:DabajianMountain.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/skins-1.18/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Dabajian_Mountain" title="Dabajian Mountain">Dabajian Mountain</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="rellink relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Geology_of_Taiwan" title="Geology of Taiwan">Geology of Taiwan</a></div>
<p>The island of Taiwan lies in a complex <a href="/wiki/Tectonics" title="Tectonics">tectonic</a> area between the <a href="/wiki/Yangtze_Plate" title="Yangtze Plate">Yangtze Plate</a> to the west and north, the <a href="/wiki/Okinawa_Plate" title="Okinawa Plate">Okinawa Plate</a> on the north-east, and the <a href="/wiki/Philippine_Mobile_Belt" title="Philippine Mobile Belt">Philippine Mobile Belt</a> on the east and south. The upper part of the crust on the island is primarily made up of a series of <a href="/wiki/Terrane" title="Terrane">terranes</a>, mostly old <a href="/wiki/Island_arc" title="Island arc">island arcs</a> which have been forced together by the collision of the forerunners of the <a href="/wiki/Eurasian_Plate" title="Eurasian Plate">Eurasian Plate</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Philippine_Sea_Plate" title="Philippine Sea Plate">Philippine Sea Plate</a>. These have been further uplifted as a result of the detachment of a portion of the Eurasian Plate as it was <a href="/wiki/Subduction" title="Subduction">subducted</a> beneath remnants of the Philippine Sea Plate, a process which left the crust under Taiwan more buoyant.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48"><span>[</span>49<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The east and south of Taiwan are a complex system of belts formed by, and part of the zone of, active collision between the <a href="/w/index.php?title=North_Luzon_Trough&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="North Luzon Trough (page does not exist)">North Luzon Trough</a> portion of the <a href="/wiki/Luzon_Volcanic_Arc" title="Luzon Volcanic Arc">Luzon Volcanic Arc</a> and South China, where accreted portions of the <a href="/wiki/Luzon_Volcanic_Arc" title="Luzon Volcanic Arc">Luzon Arc</a> and Luzon forearc form the <a href="/wiki/Haian_Range" title="Haian Range">eastern Coastal Range</a> and parallel inland <a href="/wiki/Huatung_Valley" title="Huatung Valley">Longitudinal Valley</a> of Taiwan respectively.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49"><span>[</span>50<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The major seismic faults in Taiwan correspond to the various suture zones between the various terranes. These have produced major quakes throughout the history of the island. On 21 September 1999, a 7.3 quake known as the "<a href="/wiki/1999_Jiji_earthquake" title="1999 Jiji earthquake" class="mw-redirect">921 earthquake</a>" occurred. The <a href="/wiki/Seismic_hazard_map" title="Seismic hazard map" class="mw-redirect">seismic hazard map</a> for Taiwan by the USGS shows 9/10 of the island as the highest rating (most hazardous).<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50"><span>[</span>51<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>On 4 March 2010 at about 01:20 UTC, <a href="/wiki/2010_Taiwan_earthquake" title="2010 Taiwan earthquake" class="mw-redirect">a magnitude 6.4 earthquake</a> hit southern Taiwan.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51"><span>[</span>52<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3> <span class="mw-headline" id="Climate">Climate</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Siouguluan-River-Hualien-Ta.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/Siouguluan-River-Hualien-Ta.jpg/220px-Siouguluan-River-Hualien-Ta.jpg" width="220" height="293" class="thumbimage" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Siouguluan-River-Hualien-Ta.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/skins-1.18/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Siouguluan_River" title="Siouguluan River">Siouguluan River</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Taiwan lies on the <a href="/wiki/Tropic_of_Cancer" title="Tropic of Cancer">Tropic of Cancer</a>. Its southern tip lies about 10 miles further north than <a href="/wiki/Oahu" title="Oahu">Oahu</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hawaii" title="Hawaii">Hawaii</a> and roughly 25 miles further north than <a href="/wiki/Cancun" title="Cancun" class="mw-redirect">Cancun</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mexico" title="Mexico">Mexico</a>. Its <a href="/wiki/Climate" title="Climate">climate</a> is marine <a href="/wiki/Tropical_climate" title="Tropical climate">tropical</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52"><span>[</span>53<span>]</span></a></sup> The northern part of the island has a rainy season that lasts from January through late March during the northeast <a href="/wiki/Monsoon" title="Monsoon">monsoon</a>, and experiences <i><a href="/wiki/Meiyu" title="Meiyu" class="mw-redirect">meiyu</a></i> in May.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53"><span>[</span>54<span>]</span></a></sup> The entire island experiences hot, humid weather from June through September. The middle and southern parts of the island do not have an extended monsoon season during the winter months. Natural hazards such as <a href="/wiki/Typhoon" title="Typhoon">typhoons</a> and <a href="/wiki/Earthquake" title="Earthquake">earthquakes</a><sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54"><span>[</span>55<span>]</span></a></sup> are common in the region.</p>
<p>Taiwan is a center of bird <a href="/wiki/Endemic_(ecology)" title="Endemic (ecology)" class="mw-redirect">endemism</a>; see <a href="/wiki/Endemic_birds_of_Taiwan" title="Endemic birds of Taiwan" class="mw-redirect">Endemic birds of Taiwan</a> for further information.</p>
<h3> <span class="mw-headline" id="Environment_and_pollution">Environment and pollution</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Scooters_in_taipei.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/Scooters_in_taipei.jpg/220px-Scooters_in_taipei.jpg" width="220" height="124" class="thumbimage" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Scooters_in_taipei.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/skins-1.18/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Scooter_(motorcycle)" title="Scooter (motorcycle)">Scooters</a> are a very common means of transportation in Taiwan and contribute to urban air pollution.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>With its high population density and many factories, some areas in Taiwan suffer from heavy pollution. Most notable are the southern suburbs of Taipei and the western stretch from Tainan to Lin Yuan, south of Kaohsiung. In the past, Taipei suffered from extensive vehicle and factory <a href="/wiki/Air_pollution" title="Air pollution">air pollution</a>, but with mandatory use of unleaded petrol and the establishment of the Environmental Protection Agency, the air quality of Taiwan has improved dramatically.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55"><span>[</span>56<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Scooter_(motorcycle)" title="Scooter (motorcycle)">Motor scooters</a>, especially older or cheaper <a href="/wiki/Two-stroke_engine" title="Two-stroke engine">two-stroke</a> versions, which are ubiquitous in Taiwan, also contribute disproportionately to urban air pollution.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56"><span>[</span>57<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57"><span>[</span>58<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3> <span class="mw-headline" id="Natural_resources">Natural resources</span></h3>
<p>Because of the intensive exploitation throughout Taiwan's pre-modern and modern history, the island's mineral resources (e.g. coal, gold, marble), as well as wild animal reserves (e.g. deer), have been virtually exhausted. Moreover, much of its forestry resources, especially <a href="/wiki/Abies_kawakamii" title="Abies kawakamii">firs</a> were harvested during Japanese rule for the construction of <a href="/wiki/Shinto_shrine" title="Shinto shrine">shrines</a> and have only recovered slightly since then. To this day, forests do not contribute to significant timber production mainly because of concerns about production costs and environmental regulations.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Camphor" title="Camphor">Camphor</a> extraction and <a href="/wiki/Sugarcane" title="Sugarcane">sugarcane</a> refining played an important role in Taiwan's exportation from the late 19th century through the first half of the 20th century. The importance of the above industries subsequently declined not because of the exhaustion of related natural resources but mainly of the decline of international market demands.</p>
<p>Nowadays, few natural resources with significant economic value are retained in Taiwan, which are essentially agriculture-associated. Domestic agriculture (rice being the dominant kind of crop) and <a href="/wiki/Fisheries" title="Fisheries" class="mw-redirect">fisheries</a> retain importance to a certain degree, but they have been greatly challenged by foreign imports since Taiwan's accession to the <a href="/wiki/World_Trade_Organization" title="World Trade Organization">World Trade Organization</a> in 2001. Consequently, upon the decline of subsistent importance, Taiwan's agriculture now relies heavily on the marketing and exportation of certain kinds of specialty fruits, such as banana, <a href="/wiki/Guava" title="Guava">guava</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lychee" title="Lychee">lychee</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wax_apple" title="Wax apple" class="mw-redirect">wax apple</a>, and high-mountain tea.</p>
<h3> <span class="mw-headline" id="Energy_resources">Energy resources</span></h3>
<div class="rellink boilerplate seealso">See also: <a href="/wiki/Energy_in_Taiwan" title="Energy in Taiwan">Energy in Taiwan</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Wind_power-Kaumei.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/Wind_power-Kaumei.jpg/220px-Wind_power-Kaumei.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Wind_power-Kaumei.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/skins-1.18/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Wind_power" title="Wind power">Wind power</a> in <a href="/wiki/Taichung" title="Taichung">Taichung</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Taiwan has significant coal deposits and some insignificant <a href="/wiki/Petroleum" title="Petroleum">petroleum</a> and <a href="/wiki/Natural_gas" title="Natural gas">natural gas</a> deposits. As of 2010<sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Taiwan&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, <a href="/wiki/Petroleum" title="Petroleum">oil</a> accounts for 49.0% of the total energy consumption. <a href="/wiki/Coal" title="Coal">Coal</a> comes next with 32.1%, followed by <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_power" title="Nuclear power">nuclear energy</a> with 8.3%, <a href="/wiki/Natural_gas" title="Natural gas">natural gas</a> (indigenous and liquefied) with 10.2%, and energy from renewable sources with 0.5%. Taiwan has <a href="/wiki/List_of_nuclear_reactors#Taiwan" title="List of nuclear reactors">6 reactors</a> and two under construction.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58"><span>[</span>59<span>]</span></a></sup> Nearly all oil and gas for transportation and power needs must be imported, making Taiwan particularly sensitive to fluctuations in energy prices. Taiwan is rich in <a href="/wiki/Wind_power" title="Wind power">wind energy</a> resources, with wind farms both onshore and offshore, though limited land area favors offshore wind resources. By promoting renewable energy, Taiwan's government hopes to also aid the nascent renewable energy manufacturing industry, and develop it into an export market.<sup class="Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources from September 2011">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<h2> <span class="mw-headline" id="Demographics">Demographics</span></h2>
<div class="rellink relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Demographics_of_Taiwan" title="Demographics of Taiwan">Demographics of Taiwan</a></div>
<h3> <span class="mw-headline" id="Ethnic_groups">Ethnic groups</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Taiwan_bunun_dancer.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/Taiwan_bunun_dancer.jpg/220px-Taiwan_bunun_dancer.jpg" width="220" height="319" class="thumbimage" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Taiwan_bunun_dancer.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/skins-1.18/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Bunun_people" title="Bunun people">Bunun</a> dancer in traditional aboriginal dress.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="rellink relarticle mainarticle">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Taiwanese_aborigines" title="Taiwanese aborigines">Taiwanese aborigines</a> and <a href="/wiki/Taiwanese_people" title="Taiwanese people">Taiwanese people</a></div>
<p>Taiwan's population was estimated in 2011 at 23.2 million, most of whom are on the island of Taiwan. About 98% of the population is of <a href="/wiki/Han_Chinese" title="Han Chinese">Han Chinese</a> <a href="/wiki/Ethnicity" title="Ethnicity" class="mw-redirect">ethnicity</a>. Of these, 86% are descendants of early Han Chinese immigrants known as the "<i>benshengren</i>" (<a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh" xml:lang="zh">本省人</span>; <a href="/wiki/Pinyin" title="Pinyin">pinyin</a>: <em>Běnshěng rén</em>; literally "home-province person") in Chinese. This group is often referred to "native Taiwanese" in English while the Taiwanese aborigines are also considered as "native Taiwanese" frequently. The <i>benshengren</i> group contains two subgroups: the <a href="/wiki/Hoklo_people" title="Hoklo people">Hoklo people</a> (70% of the total population), whose ancestors migrated from the coastal <a href="/wiki/Fujian" title="Fujian">Southern Fujian (Min-nan)</a> region in the southeast of <a href="/wiki/Mainland_China" title="Mainland China">mainland China</a> starting in the 17th century; and the <a href="/wiki/Hakka_people" title="Hakka people">Hakka</a> (15% of the total population), whose ancestors originally migrated south to <a href="/wiki/Guangdong" title="Guangdong">Guangdong</a>, its surrounding areas and Taiwan. Some of the benshengren do not often speak <a href="/wiki/Standard_Chinese" title="Standard Chinese">Mandarin</a>, but instead use their <a href="/wiki/Mother_tongues" title="Mother tongues" class="mw-redirect">mother tongues</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Taiwanese_Hokkien" title="Taiwanese Hokkien">Taiwanese</a> or <a href="/wiki/Hakka_Chinese" title="Hakka Chinese">Hakka</a>.</p>
<p>12% of population are known as <i>waishengren</i> (<a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh" xml:lang="zh">外省人</span>; <a href="/wiki/Pinyin" title="Pinyin">pinyin</a>: <em>Wàishěng rén</em>; literally "out-of-province person"), composed of people who (or whose ancestors) <a href="/wiki/Military_dependents%27_village" title="Military dependents' village">emigrated from mainland China</a> after the Chinese Civil War with the KMT government. Most Waishengren speak primarily Mandarin.</p>
<p>The other 2% of Taiwan's population, numbering about 458,000, are listed as the <a href="/wiki/Taiwanese_aborigines" title="Taiwanese aborigines">Taiwanese aborigines</a>, divided into 13 major groups: <a href="/wiki/Ami_people" title="Ami people" class="mw-redirect">Ami</a>, <a href="/wiki/Atayal_people" title="Atayal people">Atayal</a>, <a href="/wiki/Paiwan_people" title="Paiwan people">Paiwan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bunun_people" title="Bunun people">Bunun</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rukai_people" title="Rukai people">Rukai</a>, <a href="/wiki/Puyuma_people" title="Puyuma people">Puyuma</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tsou_people" title="Tsou people">Tsou</a>, <a href="/wiki/Saisiyat_people" title="Saisiyat people">Saisiyat</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tao_people" title="Tao people" class="mw-redirect">Tao</a> (Yami), <a href="/wiki/Thao_people" title="Thao people">Thao</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kavalan_people" title="Kavalan people">Kavalan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Truku" title="Truku" class="mw-redirect">Truku</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sakizaya" title="Sakizaya" class="mw-redirect">Sakizaya</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-cia_59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cia-59"><span>[</span>60<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>For sociologists, these ethnic classifications are a social construct, the contestation and compromise between political forces. Sociology scholar Wang Fu-chang writes in his book that Minnanren (Hoklo people), Hakka, Waishengren and indigenous peoples are social categories that have developed over the last fifty years.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60"><span>[</span>61<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3> <span class="mw-headline" id="Languages">Languages</span></h3>
<div class="rellink relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Languages_of_Taiwan" title="Languages of Taiwan">Languages of Taiwan</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Standard_Chinese" title="Standard Chinese">Mandarin</a> is officially recognized by the <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_China" title="Republic of China">Republic of China</a> as the national language and is spoken by the vast majority of residents. About 70% of the people in Taiwan belong to the <a href="/wiki/Hoklo_people" title="Hoklo people">Hoklo</a> ethnic group and speak both <a href="/wiki/Taiwanese_Hokkien" title="Taiwanese Hokkien">Taiwanese</a> (a variant of <a href="/wiki/Min_Nan" title="Min Nan">Min Nan</a>), as their mother tongue, and Mandarin.
