Mandarin has been the primary language of instruction in schools since the Japanese were forced out in the 1940s. The Hakka ethnic group, comprising around 15% of the population, use the <a href="/wiki/Hakka_(language)" title="Hakka (language)" class="mw-redirect">Hakka language</a>. Taiwan's aboriginal minority groups mostly speak their own native languages, although most also speak Mandarin. The aboriginal languages do not belong to the Chinese or Sino-Tibetan language family, but rather to the <a href="/wiki/Austronesian_languages" title="Austronesian languages">Austronesian language family</a>.</p>
<p>Although Mandarin is the language of instruction in schools and dominates television and radio, non-Mandarin languages or dialects have undergone a revival in public life in Taiwan, particularly since the 1990s after restrictions on their use were lifted. A large proportion of the population can speak Taiwanese, and many others have some degree of understanding. People educated during the period of Japanese rule (1895–1945) were taught using Japanese as the medium of instruction. A declining number of persons in the older generations only speak the Japanese they learned in school and the Taiwanese they spoke at home and understand little or no Mandarin.</p>
<h3> <span class="mw-headline" id="Religion">Religion</span></h3>
<div class="rellink relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Religion_in_Taiwan" title="Religion in Taiwan">Religion in Taiwan</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Confucius_temple_Kaohsiung_amk.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/Confucius_temple_Kaohsiung_amk.jpg/220px-Confucius_temple_Kaohsiung_amk.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Confucius_temple_Kaohsiung_amk.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/skins-1.18/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Confucius" title="Confucius">Confucius</a> temple at <a href="/wiki/Lotus_Lake" title="Lotus Lake">Lotus Lake</a> in <a href="/wiki/Kaohsiung" title="Kaohsiung">Kaohsiung</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Constitution_of_the_Republic_of_China" title="Constitution of the Republic of China">Constitution of the Republic of China</a> protects people's <a href="/wiki/Freedom_of_religion" title="Freedom of religion">freedom of religion</a> and the practices of belief. Over 93% of Taiwanese are adherents of a combination of the <a href="/wiki/Polytheistic" title="Polytheistic" class="mw-redirect">polytheistic</a> <a href="/wiki/Chinese_folk_religion" title="Chinese folk religion">ancient Chinese religion</a>, <a href="/wiki/Buddhism" title="Buddhism">Buddhism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Confucianism" title="Confucianism">Confucianism</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Taoism" title="Taoism">Taoism</a>; 4.5% are adherents of <a href="/wiki/Christianity" title="Christianity">Christianity</a>, which includes <a href="/wiki/Protestants" title="Protestants" class="mw-redirect">Protestants</a>, <a href="/wiki/Catholics" title="Catholics" class="mw-redirect">Catholics</a>, and other, non-denominational, Christian groups; and less than 2.5% are adherents of other religions, such as <a href="/wiki/Islam_in_Taiwan" title="Islam in Taiwan">Islam</a>. <a href="/wiki/Taiwanese_aborigines" title="Taiwanese aborigines">Taiwanese aborigines</a> comprise a notable subgroup among professing Christians: "...over 64 percent identify as Christian... Church buildings are the most obvious markers of Aboriginal villages, distinguishing them from Taiwanese or Hakka villages."<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61"><span>[</span>62<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Confucianism" title="Confucianism">Confucianism</a> is a philosophy that deals with secular moral ethics, and serves as the foundation of both <a href="/wiki/Culture_of_China" title="Culture of China" class="mw-redirect">Chinese</a> and <a href="/wiki/Culture_of_Taiwan" title="Culture of Taiwan">Taiwanese culture</a>. The majority of <a href="/wiki/Taiwanese_people" title="Taiwanese people">Taiwanese people</a> usually combine the secular moral teachings of <a href="/wiki/Confucianism" title="Confucianism">Confucianism</a> with whatever religions they are affiliated with.</p>
<p>One especially important goddess for Taiwanese people is <a href="/wiki/Matsu_(goddess)" title="Matsu (goddess)" class="mw-redirect">Matsu</a>, who symbolizes the seafaring spirit of Taiwan's ancestors from <a href="/wiki/Fujian" title="Fujian">Fujian</a> and <a href="/wiki/Guangdong" title="Guangdong">Guangdong</a>.</p>
<p>As of 2009, there are 14,993 temples in Taiwan, approximately one place of worship per 1,500 residents. 9,202 of those temples were dedicated to <a href="/wiki/Taoism" title="Taoism">Taoism</a>. In 2008, Taiwan had 3,262 Churches, an increase of 145.<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62"><span>[</span>63<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2> <span class="mw-headline" id="Culture">Culture</span></h2>
<div class="rellink relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Culture_of_Taiwan" title="Culture of Taiwan">Culture of Taiwan</a></div>
<div class="rellink boilerplate seealso">See also: <a href="/wiki/Literature_of_Taiwan" title="Literature of Taiwan" class="mw-redirect">Literature of Taiwan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cultural_history_of_Taiwan" title="Cultural history of Taiwan">Cultural history of Taiwan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Music_of_Taiwan" title="Music of Taiwan">Music of Taiwan</a>,&#160;and <a href="/wiki/Cinema_of_Taiwan" title="Cinema of Taiwan">Cinema of Taiwan</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Taiwan.nch.ntnu.apo-hsu.2005-10a.altonthompson.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/Taiwan.nch.ntnu.apo-hsu.2005-10a.altonthompson.jpg/220px-Taiwan.nch.ntnu.apo-hsu.2005-10a.altonthompson.jpg" width="220" height="141" class="thumbimage" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Taiwan.nch.ntnu.apo-hsu.2005-10a.altonthompson.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/skins-1.18/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Apo_Hsu" title="Apo Hsu">Apo Hsu</a> and the <a href="/wiki/National_Taiwan_Normal_University" title="National Taiwan Normal University">NTNU</a> Symphony Orchestra on stage in the <a href="/wiki/National_Concert_Hall_(Taiwan)" title="National Concert Hall (Taiwan)" class="mw-redirect">National Concert Hall</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Taipei_101_at_night_2010.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/4/4a/Taipei_101_at_night_2010.jpg/220px-Taipei_101_at_night_2010.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Taipei_101_at_night_2010.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/skins-1.18/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Taipei_101" title="Taipei 101">Taipei 101</a> at night</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:101.tall.altonthompson.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/101.tall.altonthompson.jpg/170px-101.tall.altonthompson.jpg" width="170" height="215" class="thumbimage" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:101.tall.altonthompson.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/skins-1.18/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Taipei_101" title="Taipei 101">Taipei 101</a> was the world's tallest building from its opening in 2004 until 2010.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The cultures of Taiwan are a hybrid blend of various sources, incorporating elements of traditional Chinese culture, attributable to the historical and ancestry origin of the majority of its current residents, Japanese culture, traditional Confucianist beliefs, and increasingly Western values.</p>
<p>After their move to Taiwan, the <a href="/wiki/Kuomintang" title="Kuomintang">Kuomintang</a> imposed an official interpretation of traditional Chinese culture over Taiwanese cultures. The government launched a <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Kuomintang_cultural_policy" title="History of the Kuomintang cultural policy">program</a> promoting <a href="/wiki/Chinese_calligraphy" title="Chinese calligraphy" class="mw-redirect">Chinese calligraphy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chinese_painting" title="Chinese painting">traditional Chinese painting</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chinese_folk_art" title="Chinese folk art">folk art</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Chinese_opera" title="Chinese opera">Chinese opera</a>.</p>
<p>Since the <a href="/wiki/Taiwan_localization_movement" title="Taiwan localization movement" class="mw-redirect">Taiwan localization movement</a> of the 1990s, Taiwan's cultural identity has enjoyed greater expression. <a href="/wiki/Identity_politics" title="Identity politics">Identity politics</a>, along with the over one hundred years of political separation from mainland China, has led to distinct traditions in many areas, including <a href="/wiki/Taiwanese_cuisine" title="Taiwanese cuisine">cuisine</a> and <a href="/wiki/Music_of_Taiwan" title="Music of Taiwan">music</a>.</p>
<p>The status of Taiwanese culture is debated. It is disputed whether Taiwanese culture is a regional form of Chinese culture or a distinct culture. Speaking Taiwanese as a symbol of the localization movement has become an emblem of Taiwanese identity.</p>
<p>One of Taiwan's greatest attractions is the <a href="/wiki/National_Palace_Museum" title="National Palace Museum">National Palace Museum</a>, which houses more than 650,000 pieces of Chinese bronze, jade, calligraphy, painting and porcelain, and is considered one of the greatest collection of Chinese art and objects in the world.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63"><span>[</span>64<span>]</span></a></sup> The KMT moved this collection from the <a href="/wiki/Forbidden_City" title="Forbidden City">Forbidden City</a> in Beijing in 1949 when it fled to Taiwan. The collection, estimated to be one-tenth of China's cultural treasures, is so extensive that only 1% is on display at any time. The PRC had said that the collection was stolen and that it legitimately belongs in China, but Taiwan has long defended its collection as a necessary act to protect the pieces from destruction, especially during the <a href="/wiki/Cultural_Revolution" title="Cultural Revolution">Cultural Revolution</a>. Relations regarding this treasure have warmed recently as each side has agreed to lend relics to the other; Beijing Palace Museum Curator Zheng Xinmiao said that artifacts in both Chinese and Taiwanese museums are "China's cultural heritage jointly owned by people across the Taiwan Strait."<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64"><span>[</span>65<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Popular sports in Taiwan include basketball and baseball. Taiwan is also a major Asian country for <a href="/wiki/Korfball" title="Korfball">Korfball</a>. In 2008, Taiwan hosted the World Youth Korfball Championship and took the silver medal.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65"><span>[</span>66<span>]</span></a></sup> In 2009, Taiwan's korfball team won a bronze medal at the World Game.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66"><span>[</span>67<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/International_Community_Radio_Taipei" title="International Community Radio Taipei">International Community Radio Taipei</a> is the most listened to International Radio Media in Taiwan.<sup class="Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources from October 2009">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Karaoke" title="Karaoke">Karaoke</a>, drawn from contemporary Japanese culture, is extremely popular in Taiwan, where it is known as KTV. KTV businesses operate in a hotel-like style, renting out small rooms and ballrooms varying on the number of guests in a group. Many KTV establishments partner with restaurants and buffets to form all-encompassing elaborate evening affairs for families, friends, or businessmen. Tour buses that travel around Taiwan have several TV's, equipped not for watching movies, but primarily for singing Karaoke. The entertainment counterpart of a KTV is an MTV, being found much less frequently out of the city. There, movies out on DVD can be selected and played in a private theater room. However MTV, more so than KTV, has a growing reputation for being a place that young couples will go to be alone and intimate.</p>
<p>Taiwan has a high density of 24-hour convenience stores, which, in addition to the usual services, provide services on behalf of financial institutions or government agencies such as collection of parking fees, utility bills, traffic violation fines, and credit card payments.<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67"><span>[</span>68<span>]</span></a></sup> They also provide a service for mailing packages.</p>
<p>Taiwanese culture has also influenced other cultures. <a href="/wiki/Bubble_tea" title="Bubble tea">Bubble tea</a> and milk tea are available in Singapore, Malaysia, Australia, Europe and North America. <a href="/wiki/List_of_Taiwanese_television_series" title="List of Taiwanese television series">Taiwan television shows</a> are popular in Singapore, Malaysia and other Asian countries. <a href="/wiki/Cinema_of_Taiwan" title="Cinema of Taiwan">Taiwanese films</a> have won various international awards at film festivals around the world. <a href="/wiki/Ang_Lee" title="Ang Lee">Ang Lee</a>, a Taiwanese director, has directed critically acclaimed films such as: <i><a href="/wiki/Crouching_Tiger,_Hidden_Dragon" title="Crouching Tiger, Hidden Dragon">Crouching Tiger, Hidden Dragon</a></i>; <i><a href="/wiki/Eat_Drink_Man_Woman" title="Eat Drink Man Woman">Eat Drink Man Woman</a></i>; <i><a href="/wiki/Sense_and_Sensibility_(film)" title="Sense and Sensibility (film)">Sense and Sensibility</a></i>; <i><a href="/wiki/Brokeback_Mountain" title="Brokeback Mountain">Brokeback Mountain</a></i>; and <i><a href="/wiki/Lust,_Caution_(film)" title="Lust, Caution (film)">Lust, Caution</a></i>. Other famous Taiwanese directors include <a href="/wiki/Tsai_Ming-Liang" title="Tsai Ming-Liang" class="mw-redirect">Tsai Ming-Liang</a>, <a href="/wiki/Edward_Yang" title="Edward Yang">Edward Yang</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hou_Hsiao-hsien" title="Hou Hsiao-hsien" class="mw-redirect">Hou Hsiao-hsien</a>.</p>
<h3> <span class="mw-headline" id="Higher_education">Higher education</span></h3>
<div class="rellink relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Education_in_Taiwan" title="Education in Taiwan">Education in Taiwan</a></div>
<p>Taiwan has several major public universities, including: <a href="/wiki/National_Taiwan_University" title="National Taiwan University">National Taiwan University</a>, <a href="/wiki/National_Chiao_Tung_University" title="National Chiao Tung University">National Chiao Tung University</a>, <a href="/wiki/National_Taiwan_Normal_University" title="National Taiwan Normal University">National Taiwan Normal University</a>, <a href="/wiki/National_Tsing_Hua_University" title="National Tsing Hua University">National Tsing Hua University</a>, <a href="/wiki/National_Chengchi_University" title="National Chengchi University">National Chengchi University</a>, <a href="/wiki/National_Cheng_Kung_University" title="National Cheng Kung University">National Cheng Kung University</a>, <a href="/wiki/National_Yang_Ming_University" title="National Yang Ming University">National Yang Ming University</a>, <a href="/wiki/National_Taipei_University" title="National Taipei University">National Taipei University</a>, <a href="/wiki/National_Central_University" title="National Central University">National Central University</a>, <a href="/wiki/National_Sun_Yat-sen_University" title="National Sun Yat-sen University">National Sun Yat-sen University</a>, <a href="/wiki/National_Chung_Cheng_University" title="National Chung Cheng University">National Chung Cheng University</a>, <a href="/wiki/National_Chung_Hsing_University" title="National Chung Hsing University">National Chung Hsing University</a>, <a href="/wiki/Taipei_National_University_of_the_Arts" title="Taipei National University of the Arts">Taipei National University of the Arts</a>, <a href="/wiki/National_Taiwan_University_of_Science_and_Technology" title="National Taiwan University of Science and Technology">National Taiwan University of Science and Technology</a>, <a href="/wiki/National_Taipei_University_of_Technology" title="National Taipei University of Technology">National Taipei University of Technology</a>, and <a href="/wiki/National_Kaohsiung_Normal_University" title="National Kaohsiung Normal University">National Kaohsiung Normal University</a>.</p>
<p>The more notable private universities in Taiwan include: <a href="/wiki/Soochow_University_(Taiwan)" title="Soochow University (Taiwan)">Soo Chow University</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fu_Jen_Catholic_University" title="Fu Jen Catholic University">Fu Jen Catholic University</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chang_Gung_University" title="Chang Gung University">Chang Gung University</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tamkang_University" title="Tamkang University">Tamkang University</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tunghai_University" title="Tunghai University">Tunghai University</a>, <a href="/wiki/Yuan_Ze_University" title="Yuan Ze University">Yuan Ze University</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tzu_Chi_University" title="Tzu Chi University">Tzu Chi University</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ming_Chuan_University" title="Ming Chuan University">Ming Chuan University</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chinese_Culture_University" title="Chinese Culture University">Chinese Culture University</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Shih_Hsin_University" title="Shih Hsin University">Shih Hsin University</a>.</p>
<h3> <span class="mw-headline" id="Sports">Sports</span></h3>
<div class="rellink relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Sport_in_Taiwan" title="Sport in Taiwan">Sport in Taiwan</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Baseball" title="Baseball">Baseball</a> is Taiwan's <a href="/wiki/National_sport" title="National sport">national sport</a> and it is a popular spectator sport. One of the most famous Taiwanese baseball pitchers is <a href="/wiki/Chien-Ming_Wang" title="Chien-Ming Wang">Chien-Ming Wang</a>, who is a starting pitcher in <a href="/wiki/Major_League_Baseball" title="Major League Baseball">Major League Baseball</a>. Other notable players playing in the United States include <a href="/wiki/Chin-hui_Tsao" title="Chin-hui Tsao">Chin-hui Tsao</a> who played for the <a href="/wiki/Colorado_Rockies" title="Colorado Rockies">Colorado Rockies</a> (2003–2005) and the <a href="/wiki/Los_Angeles_Dodgers" title="Los Angeles Dodgers">Los Angeles Dodgers</a> (2007), <a href="/wiki/Hong-Chih_Kuo" title="Hong-Chih Kuo">Hong-Chih Kuo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fu-Te_Ni" title="Fu-Te Ni">Fu-Te Ni</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Chin-lung_Hu" title="Chin-lung Hu">Chin-lung Hu</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Chinese_Professional_Baseball_League" title="Chinese Professional Baseball League">Chinese Professional Baseball League</a> in Taiwan was established in 1989,<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68"><span>[</span>69<span>]</span></a></sup> and eventually absorbed the competing <a href="/wiki/Taiwan_Major_League" title="Taiwan Major League">Taiwan Major League</a> in 2003. As of 2008<sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Taiwan&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, the CPBL has four teams with average attendance of approximately 3,000 per game.</p>
<p>Besides baseball, taekwondo has become a rather mature and successful sport in recent years. In the 2004 Olympics, Mu Yen Chu and Shih Hsin Chen proudly won the first two gold medals in men's flyweight event and women's flyweight event, respectively. Ever since the 2004 Olympics, Taiwan's taekwondo potential has become extremely prominent. Subsequent taekwondo competitors such as Shu Chun Yang successfully consolidated Taiwan's taekwondo culture.</p>
<p>In 2009, Taiwan hosted two international sporting events on the island. The <a href="/wiki/World_Games_2009" title="World Games 2009">World Games 2009</a> were held in <a href="/wiki/Kaohsiung_City" title="Kaohsiung City" class="mw-redirect">Kaohsiung City</a> between 16 July and 26 July 2009. <a href="/wiki/Taipei_City" title="Taipei City" class="mw-redirect">Taipei City</a> hosted the <a href="/wiki/21st_Summer_Deaflympics" title="21st Summer Deaflympics" class="mw-redirect">21st Summer Deaflympics</a> in September of the same year.</p>
<h2> <span class="mw-headline" id="Economy">Economy</span></h2>
<div class="rellink relarticle mainarticle">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Economy_of_Taiwan" title="Economy of Taiwan">Economy of Taiwan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Economic_history_of_Taiwan" title="Economic history of Taiwan">Economic history of Taiwan</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:101.portrait.altonthompson.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/101.portrait.altonthompson.jpg/170px-101.portrait.altonthompson.jpg" width="170" height="326" class="thumbimage" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:101.portrait.altonthompson.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/skins-1.18/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Taipei_101" title="Taipei 101">Taipei 101</a> is a symbol of the success of the <a href="/wiki/Economy_of_Taiwan" title="Economy of Taiwan">Taiwanese economy</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:THSR700T.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/THSR700T.jpg/220px-THSR700T.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:THSR700T.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/skins-1.18/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Taiwan_High_Speed_Rail" title="Taiwan High Speed Rail">Taiwan High Speed Rail</a>, with trains running at above 300 km/h, links Taipei and the southern port city of Kaohsiung in just 90 minutes.</div>
</div>
</div>
p>Taiwan's quick industrialization and rapid growth during the latter half of the 20th century has been called the "<a href="/wiki/Taiwan_Miracle" title="Taiwan Miracle">Taiwan Miracle</a>" (台灣奇蹟) or "Taiwan Economic Miracle". As Taiwan has developed alongside <a href="/wiki/Singapore" title="Singapore">Singapore</a>, <a href="/wiki/South_Korea" title="South Korea">South Korea</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Hong_Kong" title="Hong Kong">Hong Kong</a>, they are collectively known as the "<a href="/wiki/Four_Asian_Dragons" title="Four Asian Dragons" class="mw-redirect">Four Asian Dragons</a>" (or "<a href="/wiki/Four_Asian_Tigers" title="Four Asian Tigers">Four Asian Tigers</a>").</p>
<p>Japanese rule prior to and during World War II brought changes in the public and private sectors, most notably in the area of public works, which enabled rapid communications and facilitated transport throughout much of the island. The Japanese also improved public education and made it compulsory for all Taiwanese citizens.</p>
<p>When the KMT government fled to Taiwan it brought millions of taels of gold and the foreign currency reserve of mainland China to the island, which, according to the KMT stabilized prices and reduced hyperinflation.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69"><span>[</span>70<span>]</span></a></sup> Perhaps more importantly, as part of its retreat to Taiwan, the KMT brought the intellectual and business elites from mainland China.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70"><span>[</span>71<span>]</span></a></sup> The KMT government instituted many laws and land reforms that it had never effectively enacted on mainland China. The government also implemented a policy of <a href="/wiki/Import_substitution_industrialization" title="Import substitution industrialization">import-substitution</a>, attempting to produce imported goods domestically. Much of this was made possible through US economic aid, subsidizing the higher cost of domestic production.</p>
<p>In 1962, Taiwan had a per-capita gross national product (GNP) of $170, placing its economy on a par with those of Zaire and Congo. By 2008 per-capita GNP, adjusted for purchasing power parity (PPP), had risen to $33,000, contributing to a <a href="/wiki/Human_Development_Index" title="Human Development Index">Human Development Index</a> equivalent to that of other developed countries. Taiwan's HDI in 2007 is 0,943 (25th, very high),<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71"><span>[</span>72<span>]</span></a></sup> and stands at 0,868 in 2010 (18e, very high), according to the UN's new calculating method ("Inequality-adjusted HDI").</p>
<p>Today Taiwan has a dynamic, capitalist, export-driven economy with gradually decreasing state involvement in investment and foreign trade. Some large government-owned banks and industrial firms are being <a href="/wiki/Privatization" title="Privatization">privatized</a>. Real annual growth in <a href="/wiki/Gross_domestic_product" title="Gross domestic product">GDP</a> has averaged about eight percent during the past three decades. Exports have provided the primary impetus for industrialization. The trade surplus is substantial, and foreign reserves are the <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_current_account_balance" title="List of countries by current account balance" class="mw-redirect">world's fifth largest</a> as of 31 December 2007.<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72"><span>[</span>73<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Taiwan’s total trade in 2010 reached an all-time high of US$526.04 billion, according to Taiwan's Ministry of Finance. Both exports and imports for the year reached record levels, totaling US$274.64 billion and US$251.4 billion, respectively.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73"><span>[</span>74<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Agriculture constitutes only two percent of the GDP, down from 35 percent in 1952. Since the 1980s traditional labor-intensive industries have steadily been moved offshore and with capital and technology-intensive industries replacing them. High-technology industrial parks have sprung up in every region in Taiwan. Taiwan has become a major foreign investor in mainland China, Thailand, Indonesia, the Philippines, Malaysia, and Vietnam. As of the end of 2003, it is estimated that some 50,000 Taiwanese businesses and 1,000,000 businesspeople and their dependents are established in the PRC.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74"><span>[</span>75<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Because of its conservative financial approach and its entrepreneurial strengths, Taiwan suffered little compared with many of its neighbors from the <a href="/wiki/1997_Asian_Financial_Crisis" title="1997 Asian Financial Crisis" class="mw-redirect">1997 Asian Financial Crisis</a>.<sup class="Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources from September 2009">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Unlike its neighbors South Korea and Japan, the Taiwanese economy is dominated by small and medium-sized businesses rather than large business groups. The global economic downturn, however, combined with increasing bad debts in the banking system, pushed Taiwan into <a href="/wiki/Recession" title="Recession">recession</a> in 2001, the first whole year of negative growth since 1947. Due to the relocation of many manufacturing and labor-intensive industries to mainland China, unemployment also reached a level not seen since the <a href="/wiki/1973_oil_crisis" title="1973 oil crisis">1973 oil crisis</a>. This became a major issue in the <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_China_presidential_election,_2004" title="Republic of China presidential election, 2004">2004 presidential election</a>. Growth averaged more than 4% in the 2002–2006 period and the unemployment rate fell below 4%. Since the global financial crisis starting with United States in 2007, the unemployment rate has risen to over 5.9% and Economic Growth fallen to -2.9%.<sup class="Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources from July 2009">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> However, Taiwan managed to move out of the crisis in very good shape. In 2010, economic growth topped 10%, the highest rate in almost 30 years; international trade jumped more than 39% to US$526.04 billion; and the job market has turned a rosy picture with most businesses set to recruit. As a result, IMF estimated Taiwan's 2010 GDP-PPP per capita at over US$34700, surpassing that of <a href="/wiki/Finland" title="Finland">Finland</a>, <a href="/wiki/France" title="France">France</a> and <a href="/wiki/Japan" title="Japan">Japan</a> all at once.</p>
<p>Leading technologies of Taiwan include:</p>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Bicycle" title="Bicycle">Bicycle</a> manufacturing, ex: <a href="/wiki/Giant_Manufacturing" title="Giant Manufacturing" class="mw-redirect">Giant Bicycles</a>, <a href="/wiki/Merida_Bikes" title="Merida Bikes">Merida</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Biotechnology" title="Biotechnology">Biotechnology</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Nanotechnology" title="Nanotechnology">Nanotechnology</a></li>
<li><a href="/w/index.php?title=Communication_%26_Network&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Communication &amp; Network (page does not exist)">Communication &amp; Network</a>, ex: <a href="/wiki/Dlink" title="Dlink" class="mw-redirect">D-Link</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Laptops" title="Laptops" class="mw-redirect">Laptops</a> and other computers and displays, ex: <a href="/wiki/Acer_Inc." title="Acer Inc.">Acer</a>, <a href="/wiki/Asustek" title="Asustek" class="mw-redirect">Asus</a>, <a href="/wiki/MSI" title="MSI">MSI</a></li>
<li>Shipping, ex: <a href="/wiki/Evergreen_Marine_Corporation" title="Evergreen Marine Corporation" class="mw-redirect">Evergreen</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Semiconductor_device_fabrication" title="Semiconductor device fabrication">Semiconductor device fabrication</a>, <a href="/wiki/TSMC" title="TSMC">TSMC</a>, <a href="/wiki/United_Microelectronics_Corporation" title="United Microelectronics Corporation">UMC</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Smartphones" title="Smartphones" class="mw-redirect">Smartphones</a>, ex: <a href="/wiki/HTC_Corporation" title="HTC Corporation">HTC</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/TFT-LCD" title="TFT-LCD" class="mw-redirect">TFT-LCD</a>, ex: <a href="/wiki/Chimei_InnoLux_Corporation" title="Chimei InnoLux Corporation" class="mw-redirect">Chimei InnoLux Corporation</a>, <a href="/wiki/AU_Optronics" title="AU Optronics">AU Optronics</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Photovoltaic" title="Photovoltaic" class="mw-redirect">Photovoltaic</a> industry</li>
</ul>
<h2> <span class="mw-headline" id="Government">Government</span></h2>
<div class="rellink relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_China" title="Republic of China">Republic of China</a></div>
<p>Taiwan is governed by the <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_China" title="Republic of China">Republic of China</a>, but is commonly called Taiwan. The Republic of China should not be confused with the <a href="/wiki/People%27s_Republic_of_China" title="People's Republic of China" class="mw-redirect">People's Republic of China</a> which governs the mainland. Both the Republic of China and the People's Republic of China consider themselves the only legitimate rulers of all of China. <a href="/w/index.php?title=Relations_between_the_two_countries&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Relations between the two countries (page does not exist)">Cross-Strait relations</a> have been tense since the Republic of China's defeat in China's 1949 civil war. The People's Republic will not diplomatically recognize any country that recognizes the Republic of China. See <a href="/wiki/Political_status_of_Taiwan" title="Political status of Taiwan">Political status of Taiwan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Legal_status_of_Taiwan" title="Legal status of Taiwan">Legal status of Taiwan</a>.</p>

<li>Cole, B. (2006). <i>Taiwan's Security: History and Prospects</i>. Routledge. <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0415365813" class="internal mw-magiclink-isbn">ISBN 0-415-36581-3</a></li>
<li>Copper, J. (2006). <i>Playing with Fire: The Looming War with China over Taiwan</i>. Praeger Security International General Interest. <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0275988880" class="internal mw-magiclink-isbn">ISBN 0-275-98888-0</a></li>

<li>Knapp, R. (1980). <i>China's Island Frontier: Studies in the Historical Geography of Taiwan</i>. University of Hawai`i Press. <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0824807057" class="internal mw-magiclink-isbn">ISBN 0-8248-0705-7</a></li>
<li>Rubinstein, M. (2006). <i>Taiwan: A New History</i>. M.E. Sharpe. <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0765614952" class="internal mw-magiclink-isbn">ISBN 0-7656-1495-2</a></li>
<li>Shirk, S. (2007). <i>China: Fragile Superpower: How China's Internal Politics Could Derail Its Peaceful Rise</i>. Oxford University Press. <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0195306090" class="internal mw-magiclink-isbn">ISBN 0-19-530609-0</a></li>

<li>Tucker, N.B. (2005). <i>Dangerous Strait: the U.S.-Taiwan-China Crisis</i>. Columbia University Press. <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0231135645" class="internal mw-magiclink-isbn">ISBN 0-231-13564-5</a></li>
